Al-Bihari on Usul, Fiqh, and Logic
Narrates al-Bihari's wit, his quote on usul vs. mantiq, and his scholarly stature and death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Bihari's correction of the analogy between Usul al-Fiqh and Fiqh, and identify his view on the role of logic in relation to Usul.

Core Idea
Al-Bihari clarified that Usul al-Fiqh is not to Fiqh as logic is to philosophy — it is more integral.

In this lesson, we delve into a witty remark by Muhabbat Allah al-Bihari that reveals his deep understanding of the relationship between the principles of jurisprudence (Usul al-Fiqh) and the substantive law (Fiqh). He corrects a common misconception by likening the relation not to that of a balance (logic) to philosophy, but to something more intrinsic. This distinction has implications for how we view the role of logic in Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy being corrected
Find the sentence where al-Bihari mentions the mistaken comparison: the relation of Usul to Fiqh is like the relation of a balance (logic) to philosophy.
The text says: 'فإن الدلائل التفصيلية بموادها وصورها من أفراد موضوع مسائل الأصول، بخلاف المنطق الباحث عن المعقولات الثانية'.
2
Understand al-Bihari's correction
He argues that the detailed evidences (adilla tafṣiliyya), with their matter and form, are individuals (afrad) under the subject matter of Usul. This means Usul directly studies the very components of legal proofs, unlike logic which studies secondary intelligibles.
The subject of Usul is the adilla tafṣiliyya considered as evidence, not the universal rules of reasoning.
3
Contrast with logic's role
Logic investigates secondary intelligibles (ma'qulat thaniya) — concepts like universality, particularity, etc. These are abstract tools applicable to any content, whereas Usul is specific to legal evidence.
Dictionaries: ma'qulat thaniya are concepts that are predicated of primary concepts (e.g., 'universal' is predicated of 'man').
4
Appreciate the implication
Usul is foundational to Fiqh in a way that logic is not foundational to any particular science. Fiqh depends on Usul for its very materials, while logic only provides formal validity.
This aligns with al-Bihari's overall project in 'Musallam al-Thubut' where he defines Usul's subject matter carefully.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن لطائفه وغرائبه - قال في مقدمة «المسلم: وليس نسبته - أي نسبة علم أصول الفقه إلى الفقه كنسبة الميزان إلى الفلسفة ، كما وهم؛ فإن الدلائل التفصيلية بموادها وصورها من أفراد موضوع مسائل الأصول، بخلاف المنطق الباحث عن المعقولات الثانية والفقه حكمةٌ فرعية شرعية، فلا يقال على المقلد؛ شخصية الإمام المحقق محب الله البهاري لتقصيره عن الطاقة والتخصيص - أي تخصيص الفقه ـ بالحسيات ؛ احترازا عن التصوف حديث محدثٌ ، نعم ، الاحتراز عن الكلام عرف معروف. قال في «المسلم»: «سمعت من بعض الشيوخ أنه لقيه رجل من البراهمة من جبال الشمال، كان عنده قوانين يفهم منها كل لسان على وجه كلي ونقله عنه البلغرامي في (سبحة) المرجان» . وهل في هذا تأييد لمذهب عباد بن سليمان المعتزلي المشهور الذي ينقله علماء الأصول في معرض حديثهم نشأة اللغة، ويفندونه ! ؟ فليبحث ذلك . وبالجملة فإن العلامة الإمام الشيخ محب الله البهاري يعد من أعظم الأئمة المتأخرين في تاريخ الإسلام له منزلته العالية في علم الأصول، ومكانته الرفيعة في العلوم العقلية : الفلسفية والكلامية والمنطقية ، ثبتت إمامته بما جادت به قريحته وسمح به فكره من الكتب والمؤلفات النفيسة، امتاز بها عن كثير ممن عرف بهذا الشان وعفى عليه الزمان . إِنَّ السَّلَاحَ جَمِيعُ النَّاسِ تَحْمِلُهُ وَلَيْسُ كُلُّ ذَوَاتِ الْمِخْلَبِ السَّبُعُ المبحث الخامس: وفاته وثناء العلماء عليه: الموت حتم، كتبه الله على جميع بني البشر، ولن تنفلت من قبضته الجبارة نفس نفخ فيها الروح ، واستسلم علامة عصره وفقيه دهره الشيخ الإمام الله البهاري للقضاء المبرم، ولبى نداء ربه في عام ١١١٩هـ.
And among his subtleties and wonders - He said in the introduction of 'al-Muslim': 'Its relation - that is, the relation of the science of Usul al-Fiqh to Fiqh - is like the relation of the balance to philosophy, as was misunderstood; for the detailed evidences with their materials and forms are individuals of the subject of the issues of Usul, unlike logic which investigates secondary intelligibles, and Fiqh is a subsidiary, religious wisdom, so it is not said to the muqallid;' The personality of the verifying Imam Muhabbat Allah al-Bihari: due to his shortcoming in ability and specification - that is, specification of Fiqh to sensibles; as a precaution against [seeing] Sufism as an innovation, yes, precaution against speech [theology] is a known custom. He said in 'al-Muslim': 'I heard from some of the shaykhs that a Brahmin man from the northern mountains met him, who had laws by which he understood every tongue in a universal manner, and al-Bilgrami transmitted it in 'Subhat al-Marjan'. And does this support the well-known doctrine of 'Abbad ibn Sulayman the Mu'tazilite, which scholars of Usul mention in their discussion of the origin of language and refute?! So let that be researched. In summary, the scholar, the Imam, Shaykh Muhabbat Allah al-Bihari is considered among the greatest of the later Imams in the history of Islam; he has a high rank in the science of Usul, and an elevated position in the rational sciences: philosophical, theological, and logical. His Imamate is established by what his mind produced and his thought allowed of precious books and works, through which he excelled over many who were known in this field and whom time has erased. 'Indeed, the weapon all people carry, but not every clawed beast is a lion.' The fifth section: His death and the praise of scholars upon him: Death is inevitable; Allah has written it upon all of mankind, and not a soul in which spirit was breathed will escape His mighty grip. The scholar of his age and jurist of his time, Shaykh al-Imam Allah al-Bihari surrendered to the decreed fate and answered the call of his Lord in the year 1119 AH.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of the life and contributions of Muhabbat Allah al-Bihari. It begins by relating an anecdote about his wit: a comparison between the relation of Usul al-Fiqh to Fiqh and the relation of a balance to philosophy, correcting a misunderstanding. The author notes that Usul al-Fiqh deals with the detailed evidences (adilla tafṣiliyya) which are particulars under the subject of Usul, whereas logic deals with secondary intelligibles (maʿqūlāt thāniya) and Fiqh is a subsidiary religious wisdom. The biography then mentions al-Bihari's modesty in limiting Fiqh to sensibles and his caution against innovations. A story from 'al-Muslim' is related about a Brahmin who had universal laws for understanding languages, which al-Bilgrami recorded; the author questions whether this supports the Mu'tazilite position on language origin. The passage then eulogizes al-Bihari as a great Imam in Usul and rational sciences (philosophy, theology, logic), praising his invaluable works. A poetic line follows extolling the uniqueness of his caliber. The section concludes with his death in 1119 AH, describing his submission to divine decree and the inevitability of death.

Parsing a Biographical Anecdote on Language Origin
Describes al-Bihari's scholarly status and relates an anecdote about a Brahmin's universal language codes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a biographical anecdote is used to question a doctrinal position in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
A single anecdote can test a theological theory about language.

Biographies of scholars often include anecdotes that serve more than mere storytelling. In this passage, the story of a Brahmin with universal language codes is presented alongside a question: does it support a Mu'tazilite view on the origin of language? This lesson guides you in parsing such anecdotes to uncover their argumentative role in Islamic scholarly discourse.

Guided Path
1
Locate the anecdote
Find the sentence beginning with 'قال في «المسلم»' and continue until 'سبحة المرجان'. This is the core anecdote about the Brahmin.
2
Identify the doctrinal question
After the anecdote, the author asks: 'وهل في هذا تأييد لمذهب عباد بن سليمان المعتزلي...؟' This explicitly links the anecdote to a Mu'tazilite theory on language origin.
The Mu'tazilite view held that language is conventional, not divinely instituted.
3
Assess the author's stance
The author does not affirm or deny the support; he leaves it as a question ('فليبحث ذلك'). This indicates he is prompting further investigation, not endorsing a position.
4
Understand the biographical context
The passage frames al-Bihari as a great scholar, especially in usul al-fiqh and rational sciences. The anecdote is thus part of a larger portrait of his intellectual environment.

Is there in this support for the school of 'Abbad ibn Sulayman al-Mu'tazili, famous, which scholars of usul transmit in the context of their discussion of the origin of language, and they refute it? Let that be researched. In summary, the scholar, Imam, shaykh, Muhabbat Allah al-Bihari is considered one of the greatest later Imams in the history of Islam. He has his lofty status in the science of usul, and his high position in the rational sciences: philosophical, theological, and logical. Commentary
This passage is a biographical sketch praising Imam Muhabbat Allah al-Bihari. It begins with a note about his personality being concise, specifying fiqh to sensory matters as a precaution against innovations. Then it narrates an anecdote from the book 'al-Muslim' about a Brahmin who possessed universal codes to understand all languages, which al-Balghrami recorded. The poetic line at the end, 'Indeed, the weapon that all people carry is not every clawed one a beast,' seems to underscore the rarity of true scholarly excellence.

The Death and Legacy of Imam al-Bihari
Records al-Bihari's death in 1119 AH and eulogizes his scholarly legacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recall the year of Imam al-Bihari's death and summarize the key elements of his scholarly legacy as presented in the eulogy.

Core Idea
Imam al-Bihari's legacy is defined by his enduring scholarly edifice.

This passage marks the conclusion of al-Bihari's biography, recording his death in 1119 AH and reflecting on the lasting impact of his work. The author uses powerful metaphors to convey the permanence of his scholarly contributions and the gratitude of the Muslim community. By engaging with this text, you will learn how biographical entries in the Islamic tradition encapsulate a scholar's life and legacy in a few poignant lines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the date of death
Locate the year in the Arabic text. Look for the phrase 'في عام ١١١٩هـ'. This is the explicit statement of al-Bihari's death year.
The Hijri year is given as 1119, corresponding roughly to 1707-1708 CE.
2
Analyze the metaphor of the scholarly edifice
The author says 'أقام صرحا علميا شامخا' (established a lofty scholarly edifice). Consider why a building metaphor is used. It implies strength, permanence, and a visible structure that benefits others.
This metaphor is common in Islamic eulogies to emphasize the enduring value of a scholar's contributions.
3
Assess the scope of his contributions
The passage acknowledges that al-Bihari's knowledge is too vast to fully capture ('بحر زاخر'). Note how the author declines to enumerate all merits, instead offering a prayer for mercy.
This rhetorical move (taḥdīth al-niʿma) highlights the scholar's greatness by implying its limitlessness.
4
Understand the purpose of the eulogy
The eulogy serves both as a historical record and as a spiritual exhortation. The author includes a prayer for al-Bihari: 'نسأل أن يتغمده بعميم فضله'. This reflects the Islamic practice of seeking mercy for deceased scholars.
Such prayers are common in biographical works (ṭabaqāt) to conclude entries with a blessing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس: وفاته وثناء العلماء عليه: الموت حتم، كتبه الله على جميع بني البشر، ولن تنفلت من قبضته الجبارة نفس نفخ فيها الروح ، واستسلم علامة عصره وفقيه دهره الشيخ الإمام محب الله البهاري للقضاء المبرم، ولبى نداء ربه في عام ١١١٩هـ. لم يفارق دنياه الفانية إلا وقد أقام صرحا علميا شامخا ، يخلد ذكره على ممر الأيام، ويشكره من أجله جميع أهل الإسلام الخواص منهم والعوام. وصفحات كتب التاريخ تشهد بثناء أهل العلم عليه وعلى جهوده، وقد نقلنا بعض ذلك في ثنايا هذه الدراسة، ولا داعي لتكراره وبالجملة فلا مطمع في استيعاب مباحثه وغرائبه ولا سبيل إلى استقصاء مآثره وفضائله؛ لأن ذلك بحر زاخر، ومهيع لا يعرف له أول من آخر، ولكننا تبركنا بذكر القليل من عطائه الجزيل، والله نسأل أن يتغمده بعميم فضله وعظيم إحسانه، ويسكنه فسيح جنته ومستقر رحمته .
The fifth topic: His death and the praise of scholars upon him. Death is inevitable, God decreed it upon all human beings, and no soul that was blown the spirit will escape from His mighty grip. The scholar of his age and jurist of his time, shaykh Imam Muhabbat Allah al-Bihari, surrendered to the irrevocable decree and responded to the call of his Lord in the year 1119 AH. He did not leave his transient world except that he had established a lofty scholarly edifice that perpetuates his remembrance through the passage of days, and for whom all the people of Islam, the elite and the common, thank him. The pages of history books bear witness to the praise of scholars upon him and his efforts, and we have transmitted some of that in the course of this study, and there is no need to repeat it. In summary, there is no ambition to encompass his discussions and rarities, no way to enumerate his merits and virtues, because that is a vast sea and a path whose beginning and end are unknown. But we have blessed ourselves by mentioning a little of his abundant giving. We ask God to envelop him with His widespread favor and great kindness, and to settle him in His spacious paradise and place of mercy.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a formal eulogy titled 'His death and the praise of scholars upon him.' It begins with a reflection on death as an inevitable divine decree, then states that Imam Muhabbat Allah al-Bihari passed away in the year 1119 AH. The author emphasizes that he left behind a towering scholarly legacy that will keep his memory alive, earning gratitude from all Muslims. References to history books attest to the praise of scholars, but the author refrains from repetition. The passage concludes that al-Bihari's knowledge is too vast to fully capture, and the author prays for divine mercy and paradise for him. This section serves as a respectful closure to the biographical account.

Key Terms
وفاته (wafātihi): His death, specifically the year 1119 AH as recorded here.

Appreciating the Author's Scholarly Legacy
Commentator praises the scholarly legacy of the author of Sullam al-Ulum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify key rhetorical elements in a biographical eulogy from the Islamic scholarly tradition.

Core Idea
True scholarly legacy is measured by lasting benefit to the ummah, not by worldly acclaim.

In this passage, the commentator of Sullam al-Ulum offers a heartfelt eulogy for the original author, highlighting the enduring impact of his work. Such tributes are common in Islamic scholarly writings, serving to preserve the memory and contributions of great minds. As you read, pay attention to the rhetorical devices used to convey respect and gratitude.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the eulogy
Who is being praised? The commentator speaks of 'he' who left a scientific edifice. This refers to the original author of Sullam al-Ulum, not the commentator himself.
The passage does not name the author explicitly; you must infer from context.
2
Extract the claims about his legacy
What specific achievements are mentioned? He erected a towering scientific edifice, earned the gratitude of all Muslims, and his legacy is recorded in historical books.
The commentator acknowledges that the author's contributions are too vast to enumerate.
3
Analyze the rhetorical devices
The commentator uses metaphors (e.g., 'vast sea'), hyperbole ('no aspiration to encompass'), and a concluding prayer. Identify how these elevate the praise.
Such devices are common in Arabic eulogies to emphasize the subject's greatness.
4
Reflect on the purpose of the eulogy
Why does the commentator include this? It establishes the author's authority and the value of the work being commented upon, while also seeking blessings through mention of the pious.
This is a standard feature in Islamic scholarly commentaries to honor the original author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يفارق دنياه الفانية إلا وقد أقام صرحا علميا شامخا ، يخلد ذكره على ممر الأيام، ويشكره من أجله جميع أهل الإسلام الخواص منهم والعوام. وصفحات كتب التاريخ تشهد بثناء أهل العلم عليه وعلى جهوده، وقد نقلنا بعض ذلك في ثنايا هذه الدراسة، ولا داعي لتكراره وبالجملة فلا مطمع في استيعاب مباحثه وغرائبه ولا سبيل إلى استقصاء مآثره وفضائله؛ لأن ذلك 6 بحر زاخر، ومهيع لا يعرف له أول من آخر، ولكننا تبركنا بذكر القليل من عطائه الجزيل، والله نسأل أن يتغمده بعميم فضله وعظيم إحسانه، ويسكنه فسيح جنته ومستقر رحمته .
He did not depart his ephemeral world except after erecting a towering scientific edifice that perpetuates his mention over the passage of days, and for which all the people of Islam, elite and common, thank him. The pages of historical books testify to the praise of the people of knowledge upon him and his efforts, and we have transmitted some of that in the course of this study, and there is no need to repeat it. In summary, there is no aspiration to encompass all his discussions and rarities, nor any way to enumerate his legacies and virtues; because that is a vast sea and a path whose beginning and end are unknown. But we have blessed ourselves by mentioning a little of his abundant giving, and we ask Allah to envelop him with His comprehensive favor and great beneficence, and to settle him in His spacious paradise and the abode of His mercy.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a biographical eulogy from the commentator, praising the original author of the matn (likely al-Akhdari). The commentator uses rhetorical flourishes to highlight the author's immense scholarly legacy. He begins by stating that the author did not leave this world without establishing a towering scientific edifice that perpetuates his fame and earns the gratitude of all Muslims, elite and common. He then cites historical books as witnesses to the praise of scholars for the author and his efforts, acknowledging that some of this has already been transmitted in this study. He admits that it is impossible to fully encompass the author's discussions and rarities or to enumerate his legacies and virtues, describing them as a vast sea and a path with no known beginning or end. Finally, he concludes with a prayer, asking Allah to envelop the author with His favor and beneficence and to settle him in paradise.

بحر زاخر (vast sea)
عطائه الجزيل (abundant giving)

The Purpose of a Biographical Introduction
Author introduces a biographical section about the commentator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the function of a biographical introduction (nubdha yasira) in an Islamic scholarly work and how it establishes the commentator's authority.

Core Idea
Biographical introductions ground the commentary in a chain of reliable transmission.

Many classical Islamic works include brief biographical notes about the author or commentator before diving into the subject matter. This passage marks the beginning of the third chapter, which promises a 'brief summary about the commentator.' Understanding why such a summary is placed here helps you see how Islamic scholarship values the person behind the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chapter heading
Notice the phrase 'الباب الثالث' (third chapter). Why does the author announce a new chapter here? What does the chapter title tell you about the content?
The chapter heading signals a shift in focus to the commentator's background.
2
Examine the phrase 'نبذة يسيرة'
The phrase means 'a brief summary.' Why would the author specify that the summary is brief? How does this set expectations for the reader?
The author is intentionally concise, perhaps assuming the reader already knows some basics.
3
Determine the subject of the summary
The summary is 'about the commentator' (عن الشارح). What kind of information might be included? Think of teachers, works, methodology, or death date.
Such information helps assess the commentator's scholarly standing.
4
Connect to the scholarly tradition
In Islamic learning, knowing the commentator's background helps evaluate the commentary's reliability. How does a biographical note serve as a form of 'tazkiyah' (scholarly certification)?
This practice is common in hadith, tafsir, and fiqh works.

K?r... The third chapter: A brief summary about the commentator.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a new section, presumably the third chapter of the work, as indicated by 'الباب الثالث'. The author informs us that its content is a 'brief summary about the commentator' (نبذة يسيرة عن الشارح). In typical Islamic scholarly works, such a biographical note serves to introduce the commentator's credentials, teachers, and perhaps his methodology. This information helps the reader understand the authority behind the commentary. Reading a Scholarly Biography: Name, Lineage, and Titles
Biographical introduction of the author, his name, lineage, and scholarly titles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of a classical Islamic scholarly biography (name, lineage, titles, affiliations) and explain how they establish the author's authority.

Core Idea
A scholarly biography is not trivia—it's a legitimacy claim.

In the Islamic scholarly tradition, the introduction of an author is a carefully crafted statement of authority. The passage you are about to read is not merely a name and date; it is a dossier that positions the author within a lineage, a school, and a hierarchy of knowledge. By learning to decode these elements, you will understand how the tradition certifies its authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's full name and lineage
Look for the chain of names (son of...). This establishes scholarly ancestry.
The author's lineage is traced back four generations, ending with 'Abd al-Halim al-Ansari.
2
Extract the geographical and school affiliations
Find place names and madhhab/creed labels. These locate the author in time, space, and legal/theological tradition.
Geographical: al-Sahalawi, al-Lakhnawi, al-Hindi. School: Maturidi creed, Hanafi fiqh.
3
List the honorifics and titles
Note phrases like 'al-'Allamah', 'Bahr al-'Ulum'. These signal the author's reputation and scope.
Titles: Bahr al-'Ulum (Ocean of Sciences) and Malik al-'Ulama (King of Scholars).
4
Analyze the list of mastered sciences
The passage enumerates fiqh, usul, kalam, etc. This shows the breadth of authority.
Mastery is claimed in jurisprudence, principles, theology, philosophy, logic, exegesis, hadith, Sufism, and Arabic sciences.
5
Synthesize the overall claim of authority
Combine all elements to see how the passage makes a case for the author's unmatched knowledge.
The concluding phrase 'without peer in knowledge, research, and success' caps the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو العلامة الإمام الهمام، علم الأعلام، عمدة الأنام، الشيخ عبد العلي بن العلامة الشيخ نظام الدين بن قطب الدين بن عبد الحليم الأنصاري السهالوي اللكهنوي الهندي الماتريدي العقيدة والحنفي المذهب، الملقب بـ«بحر العلوم»، و«ملك العلماء». أستاذ الأساتذة، وإمام الجهابذة، علم شهير من أعلام الإسلام في الهند في القرنين الثاني عشر والثالث عشر، إمام الفقه والأصول، ورأس في الكلام والفلسفة والمنطق، ومرجع في التفسير والحديث والتصوف، وعمدة في علوم اللغة العربية كلها، معدوم النظير في الاطلاع والتحقيق والتوفيق.
He is the eminent scholar, the noble imam, the banner of the notable, the support of mankind, Shaykh 'Abd al-'Ali son of the eminent scholar Shaykh Nizam al-Din son of Qutb al-Din son of 'Abd al-Halim al-Ansari al-Sahalawi al-Lakhnawi al-Hindi al-Maturidi in creed and al-Hanafi in school, titled 'Bahr al-Ulum' (Ocean of Sciences) and 'Malik al-'Ulama' (King of Scholars). Professor of professors, imam of the brilliant, a renowned scholar among the luminaries of Islam in India in the twelfth and thirteenth centuries, an imam in jurisprudence and principles, a leader in theology, philosophy, and logic, a reference in exegesis, hadith, and Sufism, a pillar in all Arabic language sciences, without peer in knowledge, research, and success.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a biographical introduction that presents the author's full name, lineage, religious affiliation, and scholarly titles. It begins by lauding him with honorifics such as 'al-‘Allāmah' (the exceptionally learned), 'al-Imām' (the leader), and 'al-Hammām' (the high-minded). Then it gives his name: Shaykh 'Abd al-‘Alī. His lineage is traced back through his father, the scholar Nizām al-Dīn, then Qutb al-Dīn, to 'Abd al-Ḥalīm al-Ansārī, indicating a noble scholarly ancestry. His geographical origin is al-Sahālawī al-Lakhnawī from India, and his theological school is Māturīdī in creed and Ḥanafī in jurisprudence. He is given two notable titles: 'Bahr al-‘Ulūm' (Ocean of Sciences) and 'Malik al-‘Ulamā' (King of Scholars). The passage then emphasizes his extraordinary scholarly stature: he was a teacher of teachers, an imam of the brilliant, and a famous scholar in India during the 12th and 13th centuries AH. It lists his mastery in multiple Islamic sciences: fiqh (jurisprudence), uṣūl (principles), kalām (theology), falsafah (philosophy), manṭiq (logic), tafsīr (exegesis), ḥadīth, and taṣawwuf (Sufism), as well as all Arabic language sciences. The passage concludes by asserting his matchlessness in knowledge (iṭṭilā‘), research (taḥqīq), and success (tawfīq). This kind of introduction is typical in Islamic scholarly works to establish the authority of the author.

Key Terms
العلامة
بحر العلوم
الماتريدي العقيدة
الحنفي المذهب

Reading a Scholarly Biography: Name, Lineage, and Titles
Presents the full name, lineage, and honorific titles of the author Bahr Ulum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a classical scholar's full name, trace the lineage through patronymics, and differentiate between a given name (ism) and honorific titles (laqab).

Core Idea
A scholar's name is a chain of authority; each component signals creed, school, and status.

When you open a classical Islamic text, the first thing you encounter is often a string of names and titles describing the author. This passage introduces the great scholar Bahr Ulum with a cascade of honorifics, a full genealogical lineage, and affiliations. Learning to decode this information is the first step in assessing the authority behind any work. This lesson will walk you through each layer of a scholarly biography.

Guided Path
1
Locate the given name (ism) in the string
In Arabic names, the given name usually appears after honorifics like 'al-Allama' or 'al-Imam'. Here, the name is 'Abd al-Ali'. Note that 'al-Allama' is an epithet, not a name.
2
Trace the patronymic chain (nasab)
Read the 'ibn' connections. The chain is: (himself) ibn Nizam al-Din ibn Qutb al-Din ibn Abd al-Halim. Each 'ibn' introduces the father. This shows the scholarly lineage.
3
Identify geographical and sectarian nisbas
The passage adds al-Ansari (tribal), al-Sahalawi, al-Lakhnawi, al-Hindi (places), al-Maturidi (theology), al-Hanafi (fiqh). These indicate his school and origin.
4
Distinguish honorific titles (laqab) from given name
Bahr Ulum and Malik al-Ulama are titles given later, not part of his original name. They reflect his reputation. The passage also lists epithets like 'al-Imam al-Hammam'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شخصية العلامة بحر العلوم الفصل الاول شخصية العلامة بحر العلوم المبحث الأول: اسمه ونسبه ولقبه هو (1) : العلامة الإمام الهمام، علم الأعلام، عمدة الأنام، الشيخ عبد العلي بن العلامة الشيخ نظام الدين بن قطب الدين بن عبد الحليم الأنصاري السهالوي اللكهنوي الهندي الماتريدي العقيدة والحنفي المذهب، الملقب بـ«بحر العلوم»، و«ملك العلماء» (۲). أستاذ الأساتذة، وإمام الجهابذة، علم شهير من أعلام الإسلام في الهند في القرنين الثاني عشر والثالث عشر، إمام الفقه والأصول، ورأس في الكلام والفلسفة والمنطق، ومرجع في التفسير والحديث والتصوف، وعمدة في علوم اللغة العربية كلها، معدوم النظير في الاطلاع والتحقيق والتوفيق .

He is: the scholar, the imam, the valiant, the leader of leaders, the support of mankind, Shaykh Abd al-Ali ibn the scholar Shaykh Nizam al-Din ibn Qutb al-Din ibn Abd al-Halim al-Ansari al-Sahalawi al-Lakhnawi al-Hindi, of Maturidi creed and Hanafi school, titled 'Bahr Ulum' and 'Malik al-Ulama'. Master of masters, imam of the geniuses, a renowned scholar of the luminaries of Islam in India in the twelfth and thirteenth centuries, an imam of jurisprudence and principles, a leader in theology, philosophy, and logic, a reference in exegesis, hadith, and Sufism, a pillar in all the sciences of the Arabic language, unparalleled in knowledge, verification, and success.

Commentary
This passage introduces the author Bahr Ulum, giving his full name, lineage, and honorific titles. It begins by stating that he is 'the scholar, the imam, the valiant, the leader of leaders, the support of mankind' and then lists his full name as Shaykh Abd al-Ali ibn the scholar Shaykh Nizam al-Din ibn Qutb al-Din ibn Abd al-Halim al-Ansari al-Sahalawi al-Lakhnawi al-Hindi. It specifies his theological affiliation as Maturidi and his legal school as Hanafi. He was given the titles 'Bahr Ulum' (Ocean of Sciences) and 'Malik al-Ulama' (King of the Scholars). The passage then extols his expertise: he was a master of masters, imam of geniuses, a famous scholar of Islam in India during the 12th and 13th centuries of the Hijra. He is described as an imam in fiqh and usul, a leader in kalam, philosophy, and logic, a reference in tafsir, hadith, and tasawwuf, and a pillar in all Arabic sciences, unmatched in knowledge, verification, and success. The purpose is to establish his scholarly authority and comprehensive mastery.

Analyzing a Scholar's Upbringing: Bahr Ulum
Describes the birth year, family background, and early education of Bahr Ulum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract key biographical details from a scholarly biography and assess their impact on the scholar's intellectual formation.

Core Idea
A scholar's family and curriculum shape his entire career.

Biographies of scholars are not just historical records; they reveal the chains of knowledge and the educational systems that produce great minds. In this passage, we learn about Bahr Ulum's birth, his father's monumental contribution to Islamic education, and his own early mastery. By analyzing these details, you will understand how to critically evaluate a scholar's background and its significance for the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Extract birth and family details
Note the exact year and place of birth, and the family's status. Bahr Ulum was born in 1142 AH in Lakhnau, into a family of religious and scholarly honor.
This sets the geographical and social context.
2
Identify the father's contribution
Recognize that Shaykh Nizam al-Din established the 'Nizami sequence' curriculum, which became the standard for madrasas across the Indian subcontinent. This is a key example of how a single scholar can shape an entire educational system.
The curriculum is still in use with minor modifications.
3
Trace the grandfather's influence
Note that his grandfather, Mulla Qutb al-Din, was a teacher of the famous scholar Muhibb Allah al-Bahari. This connects Bahr Ulum to a higher scholarly chain.
This shows the importance of intellectual lineage.
4
Assess the educational implications
Bahr Ulum studied only under his father and completed the entire curriculum by age 17. This suggests a focused, intensive education typical of the system his father designed.
Such early completion indicates exceptional ability and a well-structured curriculum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني مولده ونشأته: (1) أنه ولد عام ١١٤٢هـ / ۱۷۲٩م ، بمدينة «الكهنو» الشهيرة، في أسرة اعتلت عرش العز والمجد في الفضائل الدينية والعلمية؛ حيث إن والده الشيخ نظام الدين ) يعد من أركان النهضة العلمية في شبه القارة الهندية، على الإطلاق، هو الذي وضع المنهج الدراسي المعروف بـ«السلسلة النظامية» لمدارس وجامعات شبه القارة أجمع، وعليها تدور حلقات الدروس إلى الآن في تلك الديار من غير تعديل يذكر، إلا إضافة الشافعية في مدارسهم ـ كما في «مليبار» بعض الكتب الفقهية والأصولية والكلامية التي تتفق مع مذهبهم (۲) - وأما جده الملا قطب الدين الشهيد السهالوي رَحِمَهُ الله فقد سبقت الإشارة إلى بعض سيرته حين تعرضنا لترجمة المصنف محب الله البهاري ؛ إذ هو من تلاميذ الملا قطب الدين فبالجملة فإن شرف الآباء ومجد الأجداد من النعم الجليلة التي يختار الله لها من يشاء من عباده، فيكون له الأثر الطيب في تنشئة المولود وتربيته لا محالة . وأما تلقيه العلوم الدينية فكان على والده الماجد، وقد فرغ من قرائة العلوم والفنون على المنهج السائد في عصره وعمره إذ ذاك سبع عشرة سنة،
Second section: His birth and upbringing: (1) He was born in the year 1142 AH / 1729 CE in the famous city of Lakhnau, in a family that ascended the throne of honor and glory in religious and scholarly virtues; because his father, Shaykh Nizam al-Din, is considered one of the pillars of the scientific renaissance in the Indian subcontinent, absolutely, he who established the educational curriculum known as the 'Nizami sequence' for all schools and universities of the subcontinent, and based on it the study circles still rotate in those lands without significant modification, except that the Shafi'is in their schools add some books on fiqh, usul, and kalam that agree with their school (2) - As for his grandfather, Mulla Qutb al-Din al-Shahid al-Sahalawi, may God have mercy on him, we have already indicated some of his biography when we discussed the biography of the author Muhibb Allah al-Bahari; for he is among the students of Mulla Qutb al-Din. In sum, the honor of fathers and the glory of grandfathers are among the great blessings that God chooses for whomever He wills among His servants, and thus it has a good effect on the upbringing and education of the child inevitably. As for his acquisition of religious sciences, it was under his noble father, and he completed the reading of the sciences and arts according to the prevailing curriculum in his time, while his age then was seventeen years.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage discusses the birth and early life of the author Bahr Ulum. It begins by stating that he was born in 1142 AH (1729 CE) in the famous city of Lakhnau. His family was distinguished in religious and scholarly virtues. His father, Shaykh Nizam al-Din, was a key figure in the scientific renaissance of the Indian subcontinent; he is credited with establishing the standard curriculum called the 'Nizami sequence' used in madrasas and universities across the region, which continues to be used with only minor additions by Shafi'is in places like Malabar. The passage then mentions his grandfather, Mulla Qutb al-Din al-Shahid al-Sahalawi, who was a teacher to the author Muhibb Allah al-Bahari. It emphasizes that the honor and glory of ancestors are blessings that positively influence a child's upbringing. Finally, it states that Bahr Ulum studied the religious sciences under his father and completed his readings of the various sciences and arts according to the prevalent curriculum at the young age of seventeen.

Tracing the Scholarly Journey of Bahr al-Ulum
Covers his studies after father's death, forced migration, and teaching in various cities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the career stages of Bahr al-Ulum and identify the factors (patronage, conflict, invitation) that shaped his scholarly life.

Core Idea
A scholar's biography reveals the social and political forces behind the transmission of Islamic knowledge.

The life of a great scholar is not just a list of dates and places; it is a story of how knowledge is pursued, protected, and passed on. In this passage from Bahr al-Ulum's biography, we see how conflict, patronage, and invitations from rulers directly influenced where he taught and wrote. Understanding these dynamics helps us appreciate the context in which classical Islamic texts were produced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting point
After his father's death, he studied under Sheikh Kamal al-Din al-Fatahpuri, a prominent student of his father. They engaged in precise scholarly debate. Locate these details in the passage.
This shows the importance of scholarly lineages.
2
Locate the turning point
He left Lucknow under duress after a conflict between Sunnis and Shias. He had criticized Shia practices in Muharram. The passage says he never returned.
Sectarian conflict can disrupt scholarly careers.
3
Map the circuit of patronage
List each city he moved to and the patron who supported him: Shahjahanpur (Nawab Hafiz al-Mulk), Ramfur (Nawab Fayd Allah Khan), Bihar (Sadr al-Din al-Bardwani). Note what each patron provided (living, stipends).
Patronage was essential for scholars to focus on teaching and writing.
4
Analyze the result of his travels
In each place, he taught, authored books, and attracted many students. In Ramfur, the large number of students strained the patron's resources, leading to another move.
Scholarly reputation could exceed local support.
5
Synthesize the pattern
Consider what this biography reveals about the relationship between scholars and rulers. Scholars relied on patronage but also maintained independence; they moved when conflict arose or resources became scarce.
This pattern is common in Islamic history.

When his father died, he went to Sheikh Kamal al-Din al-Fatahpuri, who died in 1175 AH, and who was one of the most prominent students of his father, and he would debate with him in scientific matters with precise discussion. Then he left the city of al-Kahnu under duress, following a painful incident that occurred between Sunnis and Shias; because the Sheikh criticized what the Shias practiced of innovated customs in the month of Muharram, so their anger flared, and they tried to fight and kill him. So in the end he chose to leave this town whose people were unjust, and went to Shahjahanpur, and he never returned to Lucknow after that and never entered it. When he entered Shahjahanpur, he was received by Nawab Hafiz al-Mulk, the ruler of that region, who facilitated some means of living and comfort for him there, so he devoted himself to teaching, giving benefit, and authoring books, with full determination and peace of mind, and a great number of scholars of those lands benefited from him. After residing there for twenty years, he went to the town of Ramfur, and his arrival was seized by Nawab Fayd Allah Khan, the ruler of that region, who arranged stipends for him and his companions from among the students of knowledge. So he stayed there for four years, teaching, authoring, and correcting. Many people studied with him, and groups of scholars graduated from him from various lands. Students flocked to him from most regions, eagerly rushing to him like the thirsty to water, until the mentioned Fayd Allah Khan became unable to bear their expenses. So he wanted to leave this town, but then Sadr al-Din al-Bardwani invited him to Bihar — which is different from Bihar with a kasra on the ba — so he responded and went to him with the students and scholars who were with him, and he stayed there for a period of time teaching and authoring. Then Wala Jah Muhammad Ali Khan al-Kofamwi, who died in 1210 AH, requested him...

Commentary
This passage continues the biography, covering the period after his father's death. It first mentions his study under a student of his father, Kamal al-Din al-Fatahpuri, with whom he engaged in detailed scholarly debate. Then it narrates the sectarian conflict that forced him to leave his hometown, Lucknow. He settled in Shahjahanpur, where under the patronage of Nawab Hafiz al-Mulk, he devoted himself to teaching and writing. After twenty years, he moved to Ramfur, where Nawab Fayd Allah Khan supported him and his students. However, the large number of students strained resources, prompting another move, this time to Bihar at the invitation of Sadr al-Din al-Bardwani. The passage ends with a mention of an invitation from Wala Jah Muhammad Ali Khan al-Kofamwi.

Bahr al-Ulum's Migration and Patronage
Author recounts his own life events: marriage, migration, and reception by nobles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the key events in Bahr al-Ulum's migration and analyse the role of political patronage in his scholarly career.

Core Idea
Patronage by rulers enabled scholars to teach and write, preserving Islamic knowledge.

This passage from Bahr al-Ulum's autobiography shows how his life as a scholar was shaped by both conflict and patronage. Understanding this pattern helps us see how Islamic scholarship was sustained through relationships with political authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial conflict
What sparked his departure from Lucknow? Note the Sunni-Shiite tension and his criticism of Shiite practices.
The text says 'a painful incident occurred between Sunnis and Shiites' after he criticized them.
2
Trace his migrations
List the cities he moved to in order: Lucknow → Shahjahanpur → Ramfur → Buhar → Madras.
He never returned to Lucknow.
3
Examine the patronage
For each location, identify the ruler who supported him and what they provided (salary, madrasa, etc.).
In Shahjahanpur, Nawab Hafiz al-Mulk facilitated living; in Ramfur, Nawab Faid Allah Khan arranged positions; in Madras, Nawab Muhammad Ali Khan built a madrasa.
4
Assess the impact
How did this patronage enable his scholarly output? Consider the statement about him devoting himself to teaching and authoring.
The text mentions he 'devoted himself to teaching, benefiting others, and authoring books' under patronage.

ففي السنة نفسها كان زواجه. ولما توفي والده بدأ إلى الشيخ كمال يرجع الدين الفَتَحْفُورِي، المتوفى عام ١١٧٥هـ ، والذي كان من أبرز تلاميذ والده، وكان يباحثه في المسائل العلمية بحثا دقيقا.
ثم خرج من مدينة «الكهنو» مضطرا ، في أعقاب حادث مؤلم جرى بين أهل السنة والشيعة؛ حيث أنكر الشيخ رحمة الله على ما يقيمه الشيعة من العادات البدعية في شهر محرم فثارت ثائرتهم، وحاولوا محاربته وقتله، ففي النهاية اختار مغادرة هذه البلدة الظالم أهلها، وذهب إلى «شاهجهانُفُور»، فلم يرجع إلى «لكهنو» بعد ذلك ولم يدخلها قط، ولما دخل «شاهجهانفور» استقبله نواب حافظ الملك أمير تلك الناحية، ويسر له بعض أسباب العيش والراحة هناك، فعكف على التدريس والإفادة وتصنيف الكتب، بجمع الهمة وفراغ الخاطر، وانتفع به جم غفير من فضلاء تلك الديار .
وبعد إقامة عشرين عاما بها ذهب إلى بلدة «رامفُور»، فاغتنم قدومه نواب فيض الله خان أمير تلك الناحية، ورتب الوظائف له ولأصحابه من طلبة العلم ، فأقام بها أربع سنين ، ودرس وصنف وصحح، واشتغل عليه خلق كثيرون، وتخرج عليه جماعات من الفضلاء من سائر البلدان، وقصده الطلبة من أغلب الأرجاء، وتهافتوا عليه تهافت الظمآن على الماء، حتى عجز فيض الله خان المذكور عن مؤنتهم، فأراد أن يترك هذه البلدة، فاستقدمه صدر الدين البردواني إلى «بُهَار) - وهو غير بِهَار) بكسر الموحدة ـ فأجابه ونهض إليه مع من كان معه من الطلبة والعماء، فأقام بها مدة من الزمان يدرس ويصنف .
ثم طلبه والا جاه محمد على خان ،الكوفاموي، المتوفى عام ١٢١٠هـ، نواب الديار المدراسية إلى مدراس ، فسافر إليها مع ستمائة نفس من رجال العلم والفضل، فلما اقترب من حدود مدينة (مدراس) - وهي المعروفة بـ شَنَّاي» (Chennai) حاليا - بعث إليه الأمير بعض أبنائه وأقاربه لاستقبال الشيخ، فلما دخلها في الرابع والعشرين من شهر ذي الحجة، عام ألف ومائتين وخمسة (١٢٠٥/١٢/٢٤هـ ) ، ووصل إلى باب القصر استقبله الأمير بسائر أقاربه وأركان دولته ،راجلا فأراد الشيخ أن ينزل من فمنعه الأمير عن ذلك، وحمل المحفة على عاتقه، ودخل دار الإمارة، وأنزله في قصر من قصورها وأجلسه على الوسادة وقبل قدميه، ثم اعتاد أن يحضر لديه كل يوم، ويرسل إليه المائدة من أنواع الأطعمة، وكلما يذهب الشيخ إلى قصره يستقبله استقبالا حسنا كاستقباله يوم قدومه إلى (مدراس).
ثم بنى له الأمير مدرسة عالية ، ورتب له الوظائف، فانتقل هو ومن معه إلى تلك المدرسة، وهكذا صار الشيخ مرجعا ومآبا للمحصلين في الديار المدراسية، واجتمع لديه هناك عدد لا يحصى كثرة ، واستمر الحال على ذلك زمانا طويلا، فلما مات محمد على خان المذكور عام ۱۲۱۰هـ قام ابنه مقام والده فبالغ في تعظيم الشيخ، وكذا فعل من جاء بعده، إلى أن انقرضت الدولة الإسلامية في الديار التاملية، ووقعت تحت حكم الإنجليز المستعمر .
In that same year, he married. When his father died, he began [studying] with Shaykh Kamal al-Din al-Fathpuri, who died in 1175 AH, and who was among the most prominent students of his father, and he would debate with him on scientific matters with precise discussion.
Then he left the city of Lucknow unwillingly, following a painful incident that occurred between the Sunnis and Shiites; because the Shaykh criticized the Shiites for their innovated customs in the month of Muharram, they became enraged and tried to fight and kill him. In the end, he chose to leave this town whose people were oppressive, and went to Shahjahanpur, and never returned to Lucknow afterward nor ever entered it. When he entered Shahjahanpur, Nawab Hafiz al-Mulk, the ruler of that region, received him and facilitated some means of living and comfort there, so he devoted himself to teaching, benefiting others, and authoring books, with full determination and peace of mind, and a great multitude of the virtuous of that land benefited from him.
After residing there for twenty years, he went to the town of Ramfur. Nawab Faid Allah Khan, the ruler of that region, took advantage of his arrival and arranged positions for him and his companions among the students of knowledge. He remained there for four years, teaching, authoring, and correcting. Many people studied with him, and groups of virtuous individuals from various lands graduated under his tutelage. Students flocked to him from most regions, rushing to him like the thirsty rush to water, until the aforementioned Faid Allah Khan could no longer support their expenses. So he wanted to leave this town, and Sadr al-Din al-Bardawani summoned him to 'Buhar' - which is not Bihar with a kasra on the ba - so he responded and set out to him with the students and scholars who were with him, and he stayed there for a period of time teaching and authoring.
Then Wala Jah Muhammad 'Ali Khan al-Kufamawi, who died in 1210 AH, the Nawab of the Madras region, requested him to come to Madras. He traveled there with six hundred men of knowledge and virtue. When he approached the borders of the city of Madras - known today as Chennai - the ruler sent some of his sons and relatives to welcome the Shaykh. When he entered it on the 24th of Dhu al-Hijjah, year 1205 AH (24/12/1205), and arrived at the palace gate, the ruler received him with all his relatives and state officials, walking on foot. The Shaykh wanted to dismount from the palanquin, but the ruler prevented him from that, carried the palanquin on his shoulder, entered the palace, lodged him in one of its palaces, seated him on the cushion, and kissed his feet. Then he made it a habit to attend upon him daily and send him a table of various types of food. Whenever the Shaykh went to his palace, he would receive him with a good reception like his reception on the day of his arrival in Madras.
Then the ruler built for him a high madrasa and arranged salaries for him, so he moved with those with him to that madrasa. Thus the Shaykh became a reference and a resort for seekers of knowledge in the Madras region, and there gathered with him a multitude too numerous to count. The situation continued like that for a long time. When the aforementioned Muhammad 'Ali Khan died in 1210 AH, his son took his father's place and exalted the Shaykh greatly, and so did those who came after him, until the Islamic state in the Tamil region ended and it fell under the rule of the colonial British.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a biographical narrative detailing the life and travels of the author, Bahr al-Ulum. It begins with his marriage and his study under Shaykh Kamal al-Din al-Fathpuri after his father's death. The author then describes a conflict between Sunnis and Shiites in Lucknow that forced him to leave, highlighting his principled stance against what he considered innovated practices. His subsequent moves to Shahjahanpur, Ramfur, and Buhar show a pattern of scholarly activity under the patronage of local rulers. The most detailed part is his reception in Madras by Nawab Muhammad 'Ali Khan, who showed him great honor, including carrying his palanquin. The author emphasizes the respect he received and the establishment of a madrasa, reflecting the interconnectedness of scholarship and political support. The narrative ends with the decline of Islamic rule in the Tamil region under British colonialism.

The Author's Journey to Madras and Honorable Reception
Narrates the author's journey to Madras and his honorable reception by Prince Muhammad Ali Khan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key events of the author's journey to Madras and the honors bestowed upon him by Prince Muhammad Ali Khan.

Core Idea
A scholar's honor is proportional to the community's love for sacred knowledge.

This lesson recounts the remarkable journey of the author to the city of Madras, where he was received with extraordinary respect by Prince Muhammad Ali Khan. This episode illustrates the high esteem in which Islamic scholars were held in India, and it provides a context for the author's later work. As you read, pay attention to the specific gestures of honor and the establishment of the madrasah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the protagonist and entourage
Notice that the author traveled with six hundred learned men, indicating his scholarly stature and the importance of the journey.
The large entourage suggests a migration of knowledge.
2
Describe the prince's reception
The prince sent his own sons and relatives to greet the author, then came out walking on foot, carried the author's litter on his shoulders, and kissed his feet. These are actions of profound humility and respect.
Carrying the litter was an extraordinary honor, typically reserved for the most revered figures.
3
Note the ongoing provisions
The prince provided daily meals, built a high school (madrasa 'āliya), and appointed positions for the author and his companions, ensuring a lasting legacy of learning.
The establishment of the madrasah institutionalized the author's teaching.
4
Recognize the continuity of honor
After the prince's death, his son and subsequent rulers continued to honor the author until the fall of Islamic rule in the Tamil region.
This shows the lasting impact of the author's scholarship and the importance of supporting knowledge.

The nobles of the Madras region to Madras, so he traveled there with six hundred men of knowledge and virtue. When he approached the borders of the city of Madras - which is currently known as 'Chennai' - the prince sent some of his sons and relatives to receive the Shaykh. When he entered it on the twenty-fourth of the month of Dhu al-Hijjah, year one thousand two hundred and five (24/12/1205 AH), and reached the door of the palace, the prince received him with all his relatives and pillars of his state, walking on foot. The Shaykh wanted to dismount, but the prince prevented him from that, and carried the litter on his shoulder, entered the house of the emirate, and lodged him in one of its palaces, seated him on the cushion, and kissed his feet. Then he used to attend to him every day, and send him the table with various kinds of foods. Whenever the Shaykh went to his palace, he received him with a good reception like his reception on the day of his arrival in Madras. Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a biographical narrative describing the author's journey to Madras and the magnificent reception he received from Prince Muhammad Ali Khan. The author traveled with a large entourage of six hundred learned men. Upon his approach, the prince sent his own sons and relatives to greet him. When the author arrived at the palace gate, the prince himself came out walking on foot, accompanied by his relatives and state officials. The author intended to dismount from his litter, but the prince prevented him and instead carried the litter on his own shoulders, bringing him into the palace. He seated the author on a cushion and kissed his feet. This extreme honor shows the deep respect the prince had for the scholar. The prince then provided daily meals and had a high school built for the author, where he taught many students. After the prince's death, his son and subsequent rulers continued to honor the author until the fall of the Islamic rule in the Tamil region. This passage highlights the author's prestigious status and the high regard for Islamic scholarship in India at that time.

Assessing a Scholar's Intellectual Legacy
Author praises the prolific writing and wide influence of Bahr al-Ulum's works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify key indicators of a scholar's prolific output and influence from a biographical passage.

Core Idea
A scholar's lasting legacy is measured by their written works, their spread, and their reception.

This passage introduces Bahr al-Ulum, describing his intense dedication to writing and the widespread adoption of his works. By analyzing this description, we can learn how to evaluate a scholar's contribution to Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify personal traits
Find the Arabic phrases that describe Bahr al-Ulum's attitude towards writing. The text says 'shadid al-hirs 'ala al-ta'lif wa al-kitaba' (intensely eager for authorship) and 'utiya fi fann al-tasnif mahara qawiyya wa khibrat kabira' (given strong skill and great experience in composition). Notice how these traits ensure his remembrance.
2
Note the range of works
The passage mentions he left 'asharat al-mu'allafat ma bayn kabir wa saghir wa mutawassit' (dozens of works, large, small, and medium) across various branches, especially the intellectual and philosophical sciences. This breadth shows his expertise in multiple disciplines.
3
Assess reception and dissemination
Look for how the works were received: 'mahall i'jab al-bahithin wa al-'ulama' min dhuhuriha wa ila al-an' (admired by researchers and scholars from their inception until now) and 'intasharu fi ara' al-'alam al-islami matbu'atan aw makhtutatan' (spread throughout the Islamic world printed or manuscript). This indicates enduring influence.
4
Interpret the concluding remark
The author states that a glance at this scientific wealth tells us of the greatness of the man and his country. This connects scholarly productivity to broader civilizational impact.

The personality of the scholar Bahr al-Ulum. So here are some of what our humble research has arrived at in praise of this blessed scientific heritage:

This passage is a biographical introduction to Bahr al-Ulum, describing his character as an extremely diligent author. It highlights that he left dozens of works across various branches of knowledge, particularly rational and philosophical sciences. The passage emphasizes that these works have been admired and used by scholars from their time to the present, both in printed and manuscript forms. The editor concludes that this scientific wealth reflects the greatness of the man and his country, and presents a list of his works that follows.

Identifying Sharh and Hashiyah in Works of Bahr al-Ulum
Editor enumerates six main works of Bahr al-Ulum in logic and kalam with publication details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a sharh (commentary) and a hashiah (gloss) in Islamic scholarly works and identify their roles in the logical tradition.

Core Idea
Sharh and hashiah are two levels of commentary that preserve and refine the logical tradition.

This lesson takes you through a passage from an editor's introduction listing the major works of Bahr al-Ulum. The passage carefully distinguishes between two types of commentary: sharh (a full commentary on a base text) and hashiah (a supercommentary on a commentary). Understanding this distinction will help you navigate the commentarial tradition in Islamic logic and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text and commentary layers
In the list, find works that are called 'Sharh' (e.g., 'Sharh Sullam al-Ulum'), which are commentaries on a matn (base text). Then find works called 'Hashiyah' (e.g., 'Hashiya on Mir Zahid'), which are supercommentaries on a sharh. Note the hierarchy: matn → sharh → hashiah.
The passage also mentions 'al-Munhiyat', which is a commentary on the author's own sharh, illustrating a further layer.
2
Extract the subject matter of each work
For each numbered item, note whether it is in logic (mantiq) or kalam. For example, items 1-4 are in mantiq, item 5 is in kalam, item 6 spans general matters. This shows how Bahr al-Ulum contributed to both disciplines.
Item 3 includes an edition note about marginal texts, indicating the scholarly apparatus that often accompanies these works.
3
Distinguish sharh from hashiah by function
A sharh directly explains the original matn, often sentence by sentence. A hashiah adds clarifications, criticisms, or references to later works. In the list, 'Sharh Sullam al-Ulum' is a sharh; 'Hashiya on Mir Zahid's Risala' is a hashiah. Notice that the hashiah is built upon a sharh composed by Mir Zahid.
The editor explicitly notes that the author refers to his own hashias in his sharh, showing the interconnectedness of these genres.
4
Analyze the publication details
Examine the publication information: dates, places, and lithograph prints. This contextualizes the works historically and geographically, important for understanding the transmission of knowledge in the Indian subcontinent.
The editor mentions a manuscript copy at King Saud University, showing the ongoing scholarly value.

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the major works of Bahr al-Ulum by the editor, providing details for each. The list contains six numbered items, followed by additional notes. The first work is Sharh Sullam al-Ulum fi al-Mantiq, which the editor is currently publishing; it is one of the author's early compositions, as noted in his termination (munhiya) where he states he wrote it in his youth. The second is al-Munhiyat, which is a commentary on his own sharh. The third is a hashiah on Mir Zahid's risala in logic, with publication details and mention of marginal texts. The fourth is another hashiah on Mir Zahid's commentary on Jalal al-Dawwani's commentary on Tahdhib al-Mantiq. The fifth is a hashiah on al-Dawwani's commentary on al-'Aqa'id al-'Adudiyya in kalam. The sixth is a set of three hashias on Mir Zahid's commentaries on al-Mawaqif, dealing with general matters. The editor clarifies that these hashias are referenced in the author's own works and that a manuscript exists in King Saud University. Students should pay attention to the distinction between a sharh (commentary) and a hashiah (gloss), as these terms are used precisely.

Parsing a Scholar's Bibliography: Bahr Ulum's Works
Author enumerates his own works with publication details and cross-references.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the title, subject field, and chronological relationships of works in a classical Islamic scholar's bibliography.

Core Idea
A scholar's bibliography maps the intellectual journey through logic, theology, and usul al-fiqh.

The passage before you is not a logical argument but a list of works by the Indian scholar Bahr Ulum (d. 1225 AH). Learning how to parse such a list is a key skill in tracing the development of Islamic thought. You will learn to extract key details: the subject, publication history, and relative chronology of each work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main work
Find the first entry: Sharh al-Sullam (commentary on Sullam al-Ulum). Note its printing date, pages, and lithograph format.
2
Categorize by subject
Each subsequent entry mentions a field: 'fi al-mantiq' (logic), 'fi 'ilm al-kalam' (theology), 'fi al-falsafah' (philosophy), 'fi usul al-fiqh' (principles of jurisprudence). Assign each work to its discipline.
This helps understand the scholar's range.
3
Trace cross-references
Notice phrases like 'he referenced them in his commentary on al-Musallam' or 'it appears this book was written before his commentary on al-Sullam'. These indicate relative chronology.
Chronology aids in understanding intellectual evolution.
4
Extract publication details
For each work, note the printing place, year (Hijri or Gregorian), and publisher (e.g., al-Matba'a al-Alawiyya, Fakhr al-Matabi').
This is useful for textual history.
5
Interpret the title's hidden meaning
The title 'Fawātih al-Rahmūt' encodes the completion date (1180 AH) via abjad numerology. Recognise this common Indian practice.
Such titles reveal the author's intellectual playfulness.

Source Text

العجالة النافعة في الإلهيات، الظاهر أن هذا الكتاب قد تم تأليفه قبل شرح السلم؛ حيث فيه الإشارات والإحالات على ذلك، كما ستتطع عليه (1) ۸ - منهيات على العجالة النافعة . ۹ - حاشية على شرح الصدر الشيرازي على هداية الحكمة لأثير الدين الأبهري، في الفلسفة ، لها ثلاث طبعات: طبعة عام ١٢٦٢هـ /١٨٤٦م، بدون مكان الطبع، وطبعة المطبعة السعيدية بـ«رَامْفُوز» الهند، عام ١٣٢٣هـ / ١٩٠٥م ، وطبعة فخر المطابع ، بلكهنو / الهند ، بدون تاريخ الطبع ١٠ - فواتح الرحموت في شرح مسلم الثبوت، في أصول الفقه لمحب الله البهاري. والغالب على الظن أنه ألفه بعد تأليفه لشرح السلم؛ حيث يوجد في الفواتح إحالات على الثاني، كما سبقت الإشارة إليه قبلا ، وقال بحر العلوم في نهاية هذا الكتاب إنه سماه بـ فواتح الرحموت»، وقال إنك من هذا الاسم تعلم تاريخ اختتام الشرح ، وهذه طريقة كثير من أذكياء الهنود والعجم كما هو معلوم، تسمية الكتب بأسام تشير إلى تاريخ تأليفها، وفي إيضاح المكنون» أن بحر العلوم فرغ منه عام ۱۱۸۰هـ (٤) ، أي قبل وفاته بأربع وأربعين عاما تقريبا. وقد طبع في الهند طبعات عدة، منها طبعة مطبعة نَوَلْ كِشور.
Bahr Ulum's commentary on Sullam al-Ulum was printed at al-Matba'a al-Alawiyya in the year 1270 AH, in 54 pages, lithograph, and I have a photographed copy of this edition. 4 - Gloss on Mir Zahid Mulla Jalal, also in logic, has more than one edition: edition of 1270 AH, without place of printing, and edition of Fakhr al-Matabi' in Delhi, India, in 1336 AH / 1918 CE. 5 - Gloss on Jal al-Din al-Dawwani's commentary on al-Aqa'id al-‘Adudiyya, in theology, printed at al-Matba'a al-Mujtaba'iyya in Delhi, India, without date. 6 - Three glosses on Mir Zahid's commentary on al-Mawaqif: the old, the new, and the newest on general matters; he referenced them in his commentary on al-Musallam, so they are prior to it. Perhaps they are what he meant by his statement in the Munhiya of his commentary on al-Sullam: '... we proved it in our glosses on the commentary on al-Mawaqif' and '... we spoke about it in the glosses on the commentary on al-Mawaqif'. Otherwise, I have not found anyone indicating that he has a gloss on the commentary on al-Mawaqif other than the gloss on Mir Zahid's gloss mentioned. As for his glosses on Mir Zahid on the commentary on al-Mawaqif, in the library of King Saud University in Saudi Arabia there is a manuscript copy of his gloss on Mir Zahid on the commentary on al-Mawaqif, without specification whether it is old, new, or newest, under number 8131, and I have a copy of it in 492 pages. 7 - Al-‘Ujalah al-Nafi‘ah fi al-Ilahiyyat; it appears this book was written before his commentary on al-Sullam, as there are references and citations to that effect, as you will see. 8 - Munhiyat on al-‘Ujalah al-Nafi‘ah. 9 - Gloss on al-Sadr al-Shirazi's commentary on Hidayah al-Hikmah by Athir al-Din al-Abhari, in philosophy; it has three editions: edition of 1262 AH / 1846 CE, without place of printing; edition of al-Matba'a al-Sa‘idiyya in Ramfur, India, in 1323 AH / 1905 CE; and edition of Fakhr al-Matabi' in Lucknow, India, without date. 10 - Fawatih al-Rahmut fi Sharh Muslim al-Thubut, in Usul al-Fiqh by Muhibb Allah al-Bihari. It is most likely that he authored it after writing his commentary on al-Sullam, as Fawatih contains references to the latter, as previously indicated. Bahr Ulum said at the end of this book that he named it 'Fawatih al-Rahmut', and said that from this name you can know the date of completion of the commentary, and this is a method of many intelligent Indians and non-Arabs, as is known: naming books with names that indicate the date of their authorship. In Idah al-Maknun, it is mentioned that Bahr Ulum finished it in the year 1180 AH, i.e., about forty-four years before his death. It has been printed in India in several editions, including an edition from the press of Nawal Kishor.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and bibliographical section, not a logical exposition. The author (or a commentator) lists the works of Bahr Ulum (the author of the main commentary on Sullam al-Ulum). The list includes: 1) His own commentary on Sullam al-Ulum (a logical text) printed in 1270 AH. 2) A gloss on Mir Zahid's work on logic. 3) A gloss on Dawwani's commentary on al-‘Aqa’id al-‘Adudiyya in theology. 4) Three glosses on Mir Zahid's commentary on al-Mawaqif (a theological work), which are referenced in his later works. 5) A work on theology (al-‘Ujalah al-Nafi‘ah) likely written before his commentary on al-Sullam. 6) 'Munhiyat' (appendices) to that work. 7) A gloss on Sadr al-Shirazi's commentary on Hidayah al-Hikmah (a philosophical work). 8) 'Fawatih al-Rahmut', a commentary in Usul al-Fiqh on Muslim al-Thubut, completed in 1180 AH. This enumeration shows the breadth of Bahr Ulum's scholarship across logic, kalam, philosophy, and usul al-fiqh. The author also provides publication details and manuscript locations, indicating a careful bibliographic survey. This kind of passage is important for understanding the author's place in the tradition and the chronological order of his works, especially the primacy of his Sharh al-Sullam.

Bahr al-'Ulum's Scholarly Works: A Bibliographical Overview
Biographical list of Bahr Ulum's works numbered 7 to 14.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject areas and publication history of seven major works by Bahr al-'Ulum.

Core Idea
Bahr al-'Ulum's bibliography spans philosophy, usul al-fiqh, and tasawwuf.

This passage introduces us to the scholarly output of the author whose work we are studying. By examining his bibliography, we gain insight into his intellectual priorities and the sequence of his compositions. The details about printing and cross-references help us trace the development of his thought.

Guided Path
1
List the works in order
Go through the passage and list each work by its number and title. There are seven works, numbered 7 through 14.
2
Identify the subject of each work
Note whether the work is on theology (Ilahiyyat), philosophy, usul al-fiqh, fiqh, or tasawwuf. For example, work 7 is on Ilahiyyat, work 9 on philosophy, works 10-12 on usul al-fiqh, work 13 on fiqh (Hanafi), and work 14 on tasawwuf.
3
Note cross-references and chronology
The passage mentions that work 7 was written before Sharh al-Sullam, and work 10 was written after it. This helps establish the relative order of composition.
Pay attention to phrases like 'apparently before' and 'likely after'.
4
Examine publication details
For each work, note the printing locations, dates, and formats (lithograph, etc.). For example, work 9 has three editions, work 10 was printed in Lucknow and Bulaq.
5
Assess the significance
Consider the author's practice of naming books to indicate completion dates (e.g., Fawatih al-Rahmut) and the evaluation of the commentary as 'one of the most beneficial'.

۷ - العجالة النافعة في الإلهيات، الظاهر أن هذا الكتاب قد تم تأليفه قبل شرح السلم؛ حيث فيه الإشارات والإحالات على ذلك، كما ستطلع عليه. ۸ - منهيات على العجالة النافعة. ۹ - حاشية على شرح الصدر الشيرازي على هداية الحكمة لأثير الدين الأبهري، في الفلسفة، لها ثلاث طبعات: طبعة عام ١٢٦٢هـ /١٨٤٦م، بدون مكان الطبع، وطبعة المطبعة السعيدية بـ«رَامْفُوز» الهند، عام ١٣٢٣هـ / ١٩٠٥م، وطبعة فخر المطابع، بلكهنو / الهند، بدون تاريخ الطبع. ١٠ - فواتح الرحموت في شرح مسلم الثبوت، في أصول الفقه لمحب الله البهاري. والغالب على الظن أنه ألفه بعد تأليفه لشرح السلم؛ حيث يوجد في الفواتح إحالات على الثاني، كما سبقت الإشارة إليه قبلا، وقال بحر العلوم في نهاية هذا الكتاب إنه سماه بـ «فواتح الرحموت»، وقال إنك من هذا الاسم تعلم تاريخ اختتام الشرح، وهذه طريقة كثير من أذكياء الهنود والعجم كما هو معلوم، تسمية الكتب بأسام تشير إلى تاريخ تأليفها، وفي «إيضاح المكنون» أن بحر العلوم فرغ منه عام ١١٨٠هـ، أي قبل وفاته بأربع وأربعين عاما تقريبا. وقد طبع في الهند طبعات عدة، منها طبعة مطبعة نَوَلْ كِشور، بـ«الكهنو» عام ١٢٩٥هـ /۱۸۷۸م طبع الحجر، كما طبع في المطبعة البولاقية بمصر في جزئين مع كتاب المستصفى للإمام الغزالي، وقامت دار البصائر في القاهرة مؤخرا بتصوير الطبعة البولاقية. وهو من أنفع شروح مسلم الثبوت. ۱۱ - تكملة شرح تحرير الأصول لابن الهمام لوالده، في أصول الفقه. ١٢ - تنوير المنار شرح منار الأصول بالفارسية، في أصول الفقه، طبع ضمن مجموعة فيها [۱] نور الأنوار لملاجيون [٢] حاشية عليه: قمر الأقمار لعبد الحليم اللكهنوي [۳] دائرة الوصول لمحمد بن مبارك شاه الهروي [٤] تنوير المنار، بلكهنو الهند، عام ١٢٩٤هـ /١٨٧٧م، في ٦٢٩ ص، طبع الحجر. ۱۳ - رسائل الأركان الأربعة في مسائل الصلاة والصوم والزكاة والحج، على مذهب الإمام الأعظم أبي حنيفة النعمان رضي لله عنه، نسبها إليه المؤرخون، وقد طبع في مطبعة علوي خان بلكهنو الهند، عام ١٣٠٩هـ /١٨٩٢م، في ۲۸۸ صفحة، وطبع أيضا في المطبعة اليوسفية، بلكهنو الهند، عام ١٣٢٨هـ / ١٩١٠م، وعندي نسخة مصورة من هذه الطبعة الثانية، وهي أيضا في ٢٨٨ صفحة بالقطع الكبير. ١٤ - شرح المثنوي المعنوي، في التصوف، نسبه إليه المؤرخون.
7 - al-'Ujala al-Nafi'a in Ilahiyyat; apparently this book was completed before Sharh al-Sullam, as it contains references and cross-references to it, as you will see. 8 - Muhiyyat on al-'Ujala al-Nafi'a. 9 - Hashiya on Sharh al-Sadr al-Shirazi on Hidaya al-Hikma of Athir al-Din al-Abhari, in philosophy; it has three editions: edition of 1262 AH/1846 AD, without place of printing; edition of al-Matba'a al-Sa'idiyya at Ramfur, India, 1323 AH/1905 AD; and edition of Fakhr al-Matabi', Lucknow/India, without date. 10 - Fawatih al-Rahmut fi Sharh Muslim al-Thubut, in Usul al-Fiqh by Muhibb Allah al-Bihari. It is most likely that he authored it after his composition of Sharh al-Sullam, as there are references in Fawatih to the second, as previously indicated. Bahr al-'Ulum said at the end of this book that he named it 'Fawatih al-Rahmut' and said that from this name you can know the date of completion of the commentary, and this is the method of many intelligent Indians and non-Arabs, as is known, naming books with names that indicate their date of composition. In 'Idah al-Maknun' (2:481) that Bahr al-'Ulum completed it in 1180 AH, i.e., approximately forty-four years before his death. It was printed in India in several editions, including the edition of Nawl Kishor Press at Lucknow in 1295 AH/1878 AD in lithograph, and also printed in al-Matba'a al-Bulaqiyya in Egypt in two volumes together with al-Mustasfa of Imam al-Ghazali, and Dar al-Basa'ir in Cairo recently photographed the Bulaq edition. It is one of the most beneficial commentaries on Muslim al-Thubut. 11 - Takmila Sharh Tahrir al-Usul of Ibn al-Humam for his father, in Usul al-Fiqh. 12 - Tanwir al-Manar Sharh Manar al-Usul in Persian, in Usul al-Fiqh; printed within a collection containing [1] Nur al-Anwar of Mala Jiyun, [2] a hashiya on it: Qamar al-Aqmar of 'Abd al-Halim al-Lakhnawi, [3] Da'irat al-Wusul of Muhammad b. Mubarak Shah al-Harawi, [4] Tanwir al-Manar; Lucknow, India, 1294 AH/1877 AD, in 629 pages, lithograph. 13 - Rasa'il al-Arkan al-Arba'a in matters of prayer, fasting, zakat, and hajj, according to the school of Imam al-A'zam Abu Hanifa al-Nu'man (may Allah be pleased with him); historians attribute it to him. It was printed at Matba'a 'Alawi Khan, Lucknow, India, 1309 AH/1892 AD, in 288 pages, and also printed at al-Matba'a al-Yusufiyya, Lucknow, India, 1328 AH/1910 AD; I have a photographed copy of this second edition, also in 288 pages in large format. 14 - Sharh al-Mathnawi al-Ma'nawi, in Tasawwuf, historians attribute it to him.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a biographical enumeration of the works of Bahr al-'Ulum (the author of the main text being commented on). It lists seven works (numbered 7 through 14) from his bibliography, providing details about their content, order of composition, and publication history. The passage begins with work 7, 'al-'Ujala al-Nafi'a' on theology (Ilahiyyat), noting that it was written before his commentary on al-Sullam, as it contains cross-references to that commentary. Work 8 is a supplement or continuation (Muhiyyat) on the same work. Work 9 is a super-commentary (Hashiya) on al-Sadr al-Shirazi's commentary on Hidaya al-Hikma by Athir al-Din al-Abhari, dealing with philosophy; three printings are listed. Work 10, 'Fawatih al-Rahmut,' is a commentary on Muslim al-Thubut in Usul al-Fiqh. The passage discusses its likely composition after Sharh al-Sullam, the naming of the book to indicate its completion date (a common practice among Indian scholars), and its various editions including a lithograph at Lucknow and a Bulaq edition in Egypt paired with al-Ghazali's al-Mustasfa. Work 11 is a completion (Takmila) of Ibn al-Humam's Sharh Tahrir al-Usul, also in Usul al-Fiqh. Work 12 is a Persian commentary on Manar al-Usul, printed in a collection with other works. Work 13 is a treatise on the four pillars of Islam (prayer, fasting, zakat, hajj) according to Hanafi fiqh, printed in two editions. Work 14 is a commentary on the Mathnawi of Rumi, in Tasawwuf. The passage serves to establish the breadth of Bahr al-'Ulum's scholarship across the Islamic sciences.

B) Fawatih al-Rahmut

D) Sharh al-Mathnawi

Key Takeaway
Bahr al-'Ulum's bibliography demonstrates his mastery across multiple Islamic sciences, with particular focus on usul al-fiqh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Mapping a Scholar's Expertise Through His Works
Author enumerates three works of Bahr Ulum with publication details from Indian press.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to infer a scholar's areas of expertise from a list of his works with publication details.

Core Idea
A scholar's bibliography reveals the range of Islamic sciences he mastered.

This passage lists three works by the scholar Bahr Ulum, with details about their subjects and printing history. By examining this list, you can see that Bahr Ulum wrote on logic, philosophy, and theology. This lesson will teach you how to use a bibliography to map a scholar's intellectual scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify each work's subject
Read the description of each work. The first is 'in logic' (fi al-mantiq), the second 'in the art of wisdom' (fi fann al-hikmah, i.e., philosophy), and the third is a commentary on a creedal text (al-Fiqh al-Akbar, i.e., theology).
Subjects are often stated explicitly after the title.
2
Note the printing locations and dates
The first work had two printings in different Indian cities. The second was printed once. The third is in Persian and its printing status is unknown. This shows scholarly dissemination.
Multiple printings suggest influence; uncertainty about printing may indicate rarity.
3
Assess the range of disciplines
Bahr Ulum wrote on Mantiq (logic), Hikmah (philosophy), and Kalam (theology). This shows he was not a specialist in only one field but a polyhistor.
In the Islamic tradition, scholars often wrote across multiple sciences.
4
Verify attribution sources
The author cites sources: for the first two works, a dictionary of Arabic publications; for the third, the commentator's own cross-reference. This teaches how to track attribution.
A scholar's works may be known from external bibliographies or internal citations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٥ - شرح ضابطة التهذيب) ، في المنطق ، وقد طبع في «مدراس» الهند، عام ١٢٧٣هـ / ١٨٥٧م ، كما طبع بمطبعة علوي خان في «لكهنو» الهند، عام ١٢٩٢هـ / ١٨٧٥م . ١٦ - حاشية على المثناة بالتكرير ورد ذكره في معجم المطبوعات العربية في شبه القارة الهندية الباكستانية فقط منسوبة إلى بحر العلوم كما يظهر، وذكر أنها في فن الحكمة، وطبعت ١٢٦٢هـ /١٨٤٦م . ١٧ - شرح الفقه الأكبر، في عقيدة أهل السنة الماتريدية، ولم يشر إليه مصادر ترجمته التي تيسر لي مراجعتها، وإنما نسبه إليه الشارح نفسه في أوائل هذا الشرح ، حيث أحال عليه في بعض المسائل، وهو باللغة الفارسية، ولا علم لي بأنه قد طبع أو لم يطبع.
15 - Sharh Dabitat al-Tahdhib, in logic, printed in Madras, India, in 1273 AH / 1857 CE, and also printed at Alawi Khan Press in Lucknow, India, in 1292 AH / 1875 CE. 16 - Hashiya 'ala al-Muthanna bi al-Takrir, mentioned in the dictionary of Arabic publications in the Indian subcontinent-Pakistan only, attributed to Bahr Ulum as it appears, and it says it is in the art of wisdom, and printed in 1262 AH / 1846 CE. 17 - Sharh al-Fiqh al-Akbar, on the creed of the Maturidi Ahl al-Sunnah, not mentioned by the biographical sources I could review, but it is attributed to him by the commentator himself at the beginning of this commentary, where he refers to it in some issues, and it is in Persian language, and I do not know whether it has been printed or not.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a biographical listing of three works by Bahr Ulum. The first work, 'Sharh Dabitat al-Tahdhib', is a commentary on a logic text, printed in Madras and later in Lucknow. The second, 'Hashiya 'ala al-Muthanna bi al-Takrir', is a gloss on a philosophical work, printed in India. The third, 'Sharh al-Fiqh al-Akbar', is a theological commentary on Abu Hanifa's creedal work, written in Persian and possibly unpublished. This list shows Bahr Ulum's range from logic (Mantiq) to philosophy (Hikmah) to theology (Kalam). The author (the modern editor) notes the sources for the first two works and expresses uncertainty about the third.

Life of the Author
